How To Build A Company With AI From The Ground Up

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·10:28 ·12.3K views · 2026-04-24
Diana Hu (Partner, Y Combinator)
AI is evolving from a productivity tool into the fundamental operating system of the startup, enabling "closed-loop" organizations that replace human middle management with a queryable intelligence layer.
TL;DR AI is evolving from a productivity tool into the fundamental operating system of the startup, enabling "closed-loop" organizations that replace human middle management with a queryable intelligence layer. By prioritizing "tokenmaxxing" (API spend) over headcount, AI-native...

TL;DR

AI is evolving from a productivity tool into the fundamental operating system of the startup, enabling "closed-loop" organizations that replace human middle management with a queryable intelligence layer. By prioritizing "tokenmaxxing" (API spend) over headcount, AI-native founders can achieve 1,000x output compared to traditional competitors.

AI as the Company Operating System

  • From Productivity to Capability: AI shouldn't be framed as a mere "copilot" for existing workflows. Instead, it represents a shift toward entirely new capabilities where a single person can build features that previously required an entire team.
  • The Intelligence Layer: Founders should treat AI as the central operating system. Every decision, workflow, and process must flow through an intelligent layer that constantly learns and self-improves.
  • Closed-Loop Systems: Unlike traditional "open-loop" companies—where decisions are made without systematic feedback—AI-native companies run as closed loops. These systems continuously monitor outputs and adjust internal processes to meet stated goals with higher correctness and stability.

Creating a Queryable, Artifact-Rich Organization

  • Total Legibility: To function as a closed loop, the entire organization must be "queryable" or legible to AI. This requires capturing every important action as a digital artifact.
      • Tactical Implementation: Use AI note-takers for all meetings, minimize private DMs and emails in favor of public channels, and embed agents across all communication streams.
  • Automated Management: When a company is queryable, agents can replace manual coordination.
      • Engineering Sprints: AI agents with access to tickets (Linear), Slack, customer feedback (Pylon), and GitHub can analyze past performance and propose future plans more accurately than human managers.
      • Performance Gains: Teams adopting these practices have reported cutting engineering sprint times in half while achieving 10x the output.

The AI Software Factory and the 1,000x Engineer

  • Evolution of Development: The highest-velocity companies are moving toward "AI Software Factories," an evolution of Test-Driven Development (TDD).
      • Human Role: Humans define the spec and the success tests.
      • AI Role: Agents generate the implementation and iterate until the tests pass.
  • Removing Handwritten Code: Some advanced teams, such as the AI group at StrongDM, have reached a point where repositories contain zero handwritten code—only specs and test harnesses.
  • The 1,000x Engineer: By surrounding a single engineer with a system of agents, founders can finally realize the "1,000x engineer" (a concept popularized by Steve Yegge), enabling individuals to build products that were previously impossible.

Structural Shifts: Eliminating Human Middleware

  • Dissolving Hierarchies: Traditional middle management exists to route information between layers. In an AI-native company, the intelligence layer handles this routing, making human middleware unnecessary and eliminating information loss.
  • The Three New Archetypes: Following the model discussed by Jack Dorsey (Block), AI-native companies will consist of three specific roles:

1. Individual Contributors (ICs): Builders across all functions (Eng, Ops, Support) who use prototypes instead of pitch decks.

2. Directly Responsible Individuals (DRIs): Leaders focused solely on strategy and customer outcomes rather than people management.

3. AI Founders: Leaders who build and lead by example, refusing to delegate their AI strategy to subordinates.

  • Tokenmaxxing vs. Headcount: Success will be measured by maximizing token usage rather than hiring. Founders should be comfortable with "uncomfortably high" API bills because they replace the far higher costs of an inflated headcount.

The Startup Advantage

  • The Legacy Debt Trap: Incumbents face "standard operating procedures" and entrenched hierarchies that are difficult to unwind. Every attempt to go AI-native risks breaking a working legacy product.
  • The Clean Slate: Early-stage founders have a massive edge because they can build queryable systems and agent-driven workflows from day one without the need for retraining or reorganization.
  • Developing Conviction: Founders cannot outsource their AI strategy. Diana Hu emphasizes that founders must personally spend time with coding agents and tools to break their own priors about what is possible.
"The way to think about AI is that it should not be a tool your company just uses. It should be the operating system your company runs on." — Diana Hu
"Maximizing token usage, not headcount, will be the critical shift. The best companies will be the ones that are tokenmaxxing." — Diana Hu
"The company itself has to be rebuilt as an intelligence layer, with humans at the edge guiding it rather than routing information through it." — Diana Hu (referencing Jack Dorsey)
Hi, I'm Diana, and I'm a partner at YC. Over the past few months, it's become clear to me that AI is not just going to change how quickly software gets built or what workflows get automated. It's going to fundamentally change the way startups should be run, from what roles will exist to what products are possible to build. In this episode, I'm going to discuss how founders should think about building an AI native company, what roles their team should have, and what concrete internal practices they can adopt right now to move much faster. Currently, most people talk about AI in terms of productivity. They'll talk at length about how it can make engineers more productive, or say we need to add copilots to existing workflows and ship more features. This framing misses the shift we're currently seeing, which is less about productivity boosts than entirely new capabilities. The right person with AI tools can now build features that used to require an entire team or were just impossible. Thinking about AI in terms of new capabilities has several implications for how founders should run their companies. At a high level, the way to think about AI is that it should not be a tool your company just uses. It should be the operating system your company runs on. Every workflow, every decision, and every process should flow through an intelligent layer that is constantly learning and improving. What this means concretely is every important process in your company should be captured by an intelligent closed loop. A closed loop captures information, feeds it back into an intelligent system, and improves the process over time. If you've ever studied control systems, you'll be familiar with the difference between an open-loop and a closed-loop system. Open loops are control systems without feedback loops. In the old world, companies basically ran as open loops. You made a decision, executed it, and didn't always systematically measure the outcome and adjust the process. Open loops are inherently lossy. A closed loop, on the other hand, is self-regulating. It continuously monitors its output and adjusts its process to better meet the stated goal. Closed loops are extremely powerful for correctness and stability. With self-improving agents, your company should run as a closed loop. To build these closed loops, you will need to make your entire company queryable. In other words, the whole organization should be legible to AI. Every important action should produce an artifact that the intelligence at the center of the company can learn from and use to self-improve. This means recording your meetings with an AI note taker, minimizing DMs and emails, and embedding agents throughout communication of all channels. It also means building custom dashboards with everything in the company: revenue, sales, engineering, hiring, ops, everything. Here's a concrete example of how it could work. Take engineering management and sprint planning. If you have an agent that has access to your Linear tickets, all your Slack engineering channels, all customer feedback from emails or tools like Pylon and GitHub, high-level plans in a Notion or Google Doc, sales calls and recordings from daily standups, then the agent can analyze what was actually shipped in your previous sprint and how well they met customers' needs for real. From there, you can go a step further. With full visibility into what shipped, what worked, and what didn't, agents can start looking ahead. They can propose sprint plans for engineers that are way more predictable and accurate and on track. The days of eng manager status rollups that are super lossy are gone. Having managed engineering teams myself, and now seeing this across multiple YC companies, this is a game changer. What used to require constant coordination becomes legible and queryable by default. I've seen teams that do this cut their engineering sprint time in half and get close to 10x more done in that time. The overarching principle here is that to get their full capabilities, you need to provide models with as much context as you would provide an employee. When you do this, your company stops operating as an open loop, where information is fragmented and manually interpreted. It becomes instead a closed-loop system. Status, decisions, and outcomes are continuously captured and fed back into this intelligence layer. The result is a system that always has an up-to-date view of what's actually happening. There's also a new paradigm emerging for how the highest velocity companies build product: AI software factories. If you're familiar with test-driven development or TDD, this is the next evolution of that. With software factories, humans write a spec and a set of tests that define success, and then AI agents generate the implementation and code and iterate until the tests pass. The human defines what to build and judges the output. The actual code is the agent's job. Some companies have already pushed this to the point where their repos contain no handwritten code, just specs and test harnesses. StrongDM's AI team is an example of how to do this. Their end goal was a system that essentially eliminated the need for a human to write or review code, and so they built their own software factory where specs and scenario-based validations drive agents to write, test, and iterate on code until it meets a probabilistic satisfaction threshold. And it works. This is how you achieve the thousand-X engineer that Steve Yegge talks about by surrounding a single engineer with a system of agents that enable them to build things they would have never been able to build before. The era of the thousand or even 10,000 X engineer is here. One implication of building your company this way with AI loops everywhere, a queryable organization, and software factories, is that the classic management hierarchy no longer makes sense. In the old world, you needed middle managers and coordinators to route information inefficiently up and down an organization. In the new world, the intelligence layer serves that purpose. If your company is queryable, artifact rich, and legible to an AI, you should have almost no human middleware. This matters because your company's velocity is only as fast as its information flow. Every layer of human routing you can remove is a direct speed gain. A great example is what Jack Dorsey is doing over at Block. After going deep on the tools, he's come to the same conclusion many have. This is about more than just incremental productivity gains. His view is that if you keep the same org chart and management structure, you miss the shift entirely. The company itself has to be rebuilt as an intelligence layer, with humans at the edge guiding it rather than routing information through it. Going forward, Jack suggests every company will have three employee archetypes. The first is the individual contributor or IC. Basically the builder operator. This is someone who directly makes and runs things. In an AI native company, this is not limited to engineers. Everyone builds: eng, ops, support, sales. Everyone comes to meetings with working prototypes, not pitch decks. Second is the DRI, the directly responsible individual, focused on strategy and customer outcomes. This is not a classic manager. It's the person with a clear responsibility for the result. One person, one outcome, no hiding. The third is the AI founder type. This person still builds, still coaches, and leads by example. If you're the founder, this needs to be you at the forefront, showing your team what massive capability gains look like, not delegating your AI strategy to someone else. With this structure, companies will be able to get outsized results with much smaller teams. Maximizing token usage, not headcount, will be the critical shift. The best companies will be the ones that are tokenmaxxing. Think of the tradeoff this way. One person with AI tools can be the equivalent of what used to take a large engineering team at a pre-AI company. That means dramatically leaner engineering, design, HR, and admin teams. And so you should be willing to run an uncomfortably high API bill, because it's replacing what would have taken a far more expensive and inflated headcount. But don't just take my word for any of this. You cannot outsource your conviction on the power of these tools. You need to develop it yourself by actually sitting with coding agents and using them until you start to break your own priors about what is now possible to build. If you are an early stage founder, you have a huge advantage in getting ahead on this. You don't have legacy systems, entrenched org charts, or thousands of people to retrain. You are small enough to build your company right from day one. The opposite is the case for existing companies. They have to maintain and grow a live product while unwinding years of standard operating procedures and core assumptions about how software gets built. Some companies can achieve this by spinning up small internal skunkwork teams that can build AI native systems from scratch, separate from the core business. Mutiny is a great example of this. But for most, every change to their core processes risks breaking something that already works. So by their nature, these large companies will have a much harder time going AI native. Startups don't have that constraint, and that's a major edge to take advantage of. You can design your systems, workflows, and culture around AI from the start, and as a result, operate a thousand times faster than the incumbent.

Who Is John Ternus, Apple's New C.E.O.?

Hard Fork(57.2K) ·71:55 ·1.9K views · 2026-04-24
John Ternus (CEO, Apple) Tim Cook (Former CEO, Apple)
Hosts Kevin Roose and Casey Newton reflect on Tim Cook’s 15-year tenure, characterizing it as an era of unprecedented scale and logistical mastery defined by several key metrics and milestones:
TL;DR: Apple CEO Tim Cook is stepping down to become Executive Chairman, passing the mantle to hardware veteran John Ternus as the company grapples with its legacy of financial growth versus its status as an "AI laggard." Meanwhile, Andrew Yang argues that AI-driven job...

TL;DR: Apple CEO Tim Cook is stepping down to become Executive Chairman, passing the mantle to hardware veteran John Ternus as the company grapples with its legacy of financial growth versus its status as an "AI laggard." Meanwhile, Andrew Yang argues that AI-driven job displacement has reached a tipping point, making Universal Basic Income (UBI) an economic necessity.

The Tim Cook Legacy: Financial Triumph and Strategic Gaps

Hosts Kevin Roose and Casey Newton reflect on Tim Cook’s 15-year tenure, characterizing it as an era of unprecedented scale and logistical mastery defined by several key metrics and milestones:

  • Financial Explosion: Under Cook, Apple’s market cap grew from $350 billion in 2011 to approximately $4 trillion today. Revenue nearly quadrupled, and the stock price rose roughly 2,000%.
  • Hardware and Services Success:
      • Apple Watch: Initially seen as a luxury gadget, Cook successfully pivoted the device toward health and fitness, making it a mainstream hit.
      • Apple Silicon: The move from Intel to in-house M1 chips is cited as Cook’s most lasting and successful strategic bet, giving Apple total control over its hardware destiny.
      • Services Revenue: Apple built a $100 billion business out of Apple TV, Music, and Pay, though the hosts note this often came at the cost of antitrust scrutiny and "distorting the market."
  • The "Lowlights":
      • The "Next Big Thing" Gap: Cook failed to find a successor to the iPhone’s dominance; Project Titan (the $10 billion Apple Car) was canceled, and the Vision Pro has yet to find a market.
      • AI Laggard Status: Apple is currently perceived as being behind Google and OpenAI, forcing them to license models like Gemini rather than leading with proprietary frontier AI.
      • Political Pragmatism: Cook is criticized for his "spineless" appeasement of Donald Trump to avoid tariffs and for Apple's deep manufacturing dependency on China.

The New CEO: John Ternus and the "Stability" Hire

John Ternus, formerly Senior Vice President of Hardware Engineering, represents a safe, internal succession plan for Apple.

  • Background: Ternus was a key figure behind the AirPods and the transition to Apple Silicon. His appointment signals that Apple will remain a "hardware-first" company.
  • The Stability Strategy: Newton argues that as the steward of the world's most lucrative platform, Apple is prioritizing stability over radical disruption.
  • Advice for the New CEO:
      • Fix Siri: Roose suggests that turning Siri into a truly functional AI assistant would be the quickest way for Ternus to prove his leadership.
      • Consumer AR: Newton urges Ternus to abandon the bulky Vision Pro approach in favor of sleek, camera-enabled glasses similar to the Meta Ray-Bans.

Andrew Yang on the Return of Universal Basic Income (UBI)

Former presidential candidate Andrew Yang joins the show to discuss why his 2020 platform of UBI (the "Freedom Dividend") is seeing a resurgence among tech leaders like Elon Musk and Sam Altman.

  • The Changing Face of Automation: Yang admits he was early in 2018, but notes a shift: whereas he originally feared for truckers and retail workers, AI is now primarily threatening "white-collar" knowledge workers, coders, and paralegals.
  • The AI Tax: Yang supports a "token tax" on AI companies (suggesting a 3% tax, as recently floated by Anthropic's CEO) to fund UBI. He argues that since the gains of AI will be hyper-concentrated, the government must redistribute that wealth to prevent a "dog-eat-dog" economy.
  • Job Purpose vs. Income: Yang acknowledges that a check cannot replace the community and structure a job provides. He advocates for UBI as a floor that allows people to start their own non-profits or businesses rather than relying on "paternalistic" government jobs.
  • Political Reality: Yang describes the current Silicon Valley culture as "darkening," with many leaders adopting a fatalistic "bunker" mentality rather than trying to preserve the broader social fabric.

HatGPT: AI Surveillance and Industry Moves

In the "HatGPT" segment, the hosts cover rapid-fire news involving AI integration and corporate surveillance:

  • Meta’s Internal Spyware: Meta is reportedly capturing employee mouse movements, keystrokes, and screen snapshots to train autonomous AI agents. Roose notes that employees are "understandably outraged" by this move into "standard contractor-style spyware."
  • SpaceX and Cursor: SpaceX reached a deal to either acquire or pay $10 billion to the AI coding startup Cursor, signaling a consolidation in the "agentic coding" space.
  • AI in Retail: A store in San Francisco ("Andon Market") is being run entirely by an AI agent named Luna. The experiment has faced hurdles, including the AI paying male employees $2 more per hour than females and losing $13,000 in early operations.

Notable Quotes

"If you want to be intellectually honest about Tim Cook’s Apple, you have to talk about this particular dimension because the knock on Tim Cook was, 'well he's not a product guy,' but you look at the past 15 years and he actually did." — Casey Newton
"Congress has a 16 percent approval rating right now and incumbent members have a 94 percent reelection rate. It's like a restaurant where people hate 84 percent of the food but the menu never changes." — Andrew Yang
"Tax AI, tax the bots, don't tax humans... If the average American actually felt [the gains of AI] in their bank account, then they would actually be pretty positive about it." — Andrew Yang
"Meta has adopted tactics previously used only by a pasta sauce company. That’s where we’re at." — Kevin Roose (referencing Prego's new table-recording device and Meta's employee tracking).

And then I think Tim Cook to his credit saw that health was taking off, that people wanted to track their steps, they wanted to know if their blood oxygen levels were changing or if their heartbeat was irregular, they wanted to have fall detection, and I think he really saw that as the way to bring the Apple Watch to the mainstream. What Tim Cook’s “Executive Chairman” Move Really Means | Musk vs OpenAI, xAI and Cursor Rumors

More or Less Podcast(49.4K) ·52:44 ·283 views · 2026-04-24
Tim Cook (Executive Chairman, Apple) Elon Musk (CEO, xAI) Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
Apple’s leadership transition signals a strategic pivot toward a high-level capital allocation model similar to Alphabet, while the impending Musk vs.
TL;DR Apple’s leadership transition signals a strategic pivot toward a high-level capital allocation model similar to Alphabet, while the impending Musk vs. OpenAI trial highlights a growing industry debate over the "bait-and-switch" transition from nonprofit research to...

TL;DR

Apple’s leadership transition signals a strategic pivot toward a high-level capital allocation model similar to Alphabet, while the impending Musk vs. OpenAI trial highlights a growing industry debate over the "bait-and-switch" transition from nonprofit research to for-profit dominance.

Apple’s Leadership Transition: Stepping Up, Not Down

  • Executive Chairman Shift: The panel discusses Tim Cook’s transition to Executive Chairman as John Ternus (VP of Hardware Engineering) prepares to take the CEO role.
      • Strategic Framing: Sam Lessin argues that Cook is "stepping up" rather than stepping down, moving Apple toward a structural model like Alphabet or Meta where the top tier focuses on capital allocation rather than day-to-day operations.
      • Continuity and Safety: The "Executive Chairman" title suggests Cook remains available to step back in if the AI transition falters, providing a safety net for investors.
  • The Rise of John Ternus: Ternus is characterized as a "calm" leader compared to the "bombastic" personalities of Apple’s past (e.g., Phil Schiller or Eddy Cue).
      • Background: A "Marin boy" with deep roots in hardware, Ternus has recently taken on more chip architecture and public-facing responsibilities.

The Musk vs. OpenAI Legal Battle

  • The "Bait and Switch" Allegation: The upcoming trial centers on whether OpenAI had the right to convert from a nonprofit to a for-profit entity.
      • Talent and Data Poaching: Sam Lessin notes that the original pitch to researchers and the public was "open-ended good for the world," which allowed the company to scrape data and hire talent that might have avoided a "dirty for-profit" company.
  • Narrative over Capital: The panel suggests Elon Musk’s motivation is not personal wealth—as he has pledged to donate damages to the OpenAI Foundation—but rather "narrative power."
      • WWE/MMA Theory: Success in Silicon Valley is often priced on the narrative of being "bulletproof." Musk aims to prove OpenAI is "touchable" to protect his own market valuations.
  • Settlement Speculation: Jessica Lessin suggests there is a high likelihood of a settlement before the Monday trial date, as OpenAI cannot afford the distraction and Musk simply needs a way to "declare victory."

The Gold Mine of AI: "Traces" and xAI’s Cursor Rumors

  • The xAI/Cursor Deal: Rumors suggest xAI may partner with or acquire the AI coding assistant Cursor for up to $60 billion (or a $10 billion "consulting" floor).
      • The Value of Traces: Dave Morin explains that the industry is desperate for "traces"—the step-by-step data of how a human developer interacts with code, prompts, and errors.
      • Competitive Advantage: While Cursor’s standalone valuation may be shaky, its data set is "evergreen" and essential for training models to use computers (terminal, bash, etc.) effectively.
  • Meta’s Internal Surveillance: Meta is reportedly tracking employee keystrokes and screen activity to build "computer use" models.
      • The "Panopticon" Effect: While capturing "data exhaust" from paid employees is a logical extension of R&D, the panel notes it feels "creepy" and risks damaging employee morale during an era of layoffs.

The AI "Branding Problem" and the Meaning Crisis

  • Public Backlash: The panel notes a growing "branding problem" for AI, exemplified by the backlash against Reese Witherspoon for encouraging women to learn AI tools.
      • The Skeptic's View: Public concerns focus on job displacement, environmental impact (water/energy usage), and poor output quality.
  • Agency vs. Displacement:
      • The Optimist’s Case: Dave Morin argues AI provides "agency," allowing a 70-year-old in Switzerland to brew and market beer via "Agentic Computing" without needing to learn esoteric code.
      • The Realist’s Case: Sam Lessin counters that increased individual leverage means we "don't need to include as many people," potentially hollowing out the middle class and reducing the need for human collaboration.

***

Notable Quotes

"Tim Cook stepped up... he’s like, 'I created four trillion dollars of value, I’m going to kick myself up. Good luck with the AI problem. If it goes badly, I’m back.'" — Sam Lessin
"The problem is meaning. You can’t just pay people [UBI] and make them happy. They’ll just be sadder. The problem is not basic income, it’s meaning." — Sam Lessin
"The thing that gives people meaning is agency—their ability to create what they want to create... AI is going to unlock tens of millions of more people to create software." — Dave Morin
"OpenAI's story is not that someone passed you; the story is 'we’ve been telling the world we’re untouchable, and we’re touchable.' Once you can be touched once, you can be touched again." — Sam Lessin

Meet the New Thiel Fellows

John Coogan(455.0K) ·135:34 ·967 views · 2026-04-24
John Coogan (Host) Peter Thiel (Founder, Thiel Fellowship)
The latest Thiel Fellowship class is leveraging AI and custom hardware to disrupt legacy industries—ranging from autonomous logistics and small-cap equity research to government fraud detection—amidst the release of GPT 5.5 and rising national securi...
TL;DR The latest Thiel Fellowship class is leveraging AI and custom hardware to disrupt legacy industries—ranging from autonomous logistics and small-cap equity research to government fraud detection—amidst the release of GPT 5.5 and rising national security concerns regarding...

TL;DR

The latest Thiel Fellowship class is leveraging AI and custom hardware to disrupt legacy industries—ranging from autonomous logistics and small-cap equity research to government fraud detection—amidst the release of GPT 5.5 and rising national security concerns regarding AI model "distillation" by foreign entities.

The New Thiel Fellowship Class: Industry Disruptors

The fellowship continues to provide $250,000 grants to young entrepreneurs to build companies in lieu of attending college.

  • Logistics & Robotics:
      • Victor Boyd (Cavalla): Building autonomous forklifts from the ground up.
          • Hardware Strategy: Moved away from retrofitting existing machinery (the "comma.ai approach") to building custom hardware (the "Tesla approach") for better reliability.
          • Target Market: Currently deployed in pharmaceutical warehouses where pallet damage can cost hundreds of thousands of dollars.
      • Milan (Opt 32): Developing full-stack compute infrastructure for physical autonomy.
          • Full-Stack Scope: Building everything from compilers to custom chips (ASICs) to run on-device machine learning for robots and drones.
  • Information & Investigation:
      • Alex (The Antifraud Company): Utilizing AI for investigative journalism and legal action against government fraudsters.
          • Business Model: Operates on contingency, keeping 15–30% of recovered funds under whistleblower laws.
          • Scope: Monitoring $600 billion in annual government fraud across healthcare, defense, and SBA loans.
      • Nick (Everyticker): The youngest-ever Thiel Fellow, building an AI-powered equity research platform.
          • Market Gap: Provides high-quality research on 5,000+ small and micro-cap stocks that Wall Street analysts typically ignore.
  • Platform & Recruitment Intelligence:
      • Ishan Gupta (Juicebox): An AI recruiting agent that moves beyond keyword pattern matching.
          • Talent Analysis: Analyzes real work products (e.g., GitHub contributions) to find the best hires for companies like Ramp and Scale.
      • Galen Mead (Standard Intelligence): Developing "computer-use" models.
          • AI Agents: Training base models to interact with computers via screen and keyboard as a universal form factor for AGI.

Emerging Global Super-Apps and Infrastructure

  • Aubrey (Swoop): Building a "Super App" for Africa, starting with food delivery in Nigeria.
      • Financial Strategy: Uses food delivery as a low-cost acquisition wedge to convert users to high-margin peer-to-peer payment services.
  • Samuel (Praso): Building the digital infrastructure for wholesale commerce in Brazil.
      • SMB Focus: Digitizing procurement and credit for 10,000+ restaurants, providing a one-stop-shop that manages logistics and short-term (10-day) credit.
  • Claire Wang: Conducting biologically accurate simulations of nervous systems.
      • Research Goal: Simulating the *C. elegans* (worm) brain to better understand consciousness and improve brain-computer interfaces (BCI).

AI Frontier: GPT 5.5 and National Security

The tech landscape is shifting rapidly with new model releases and increasing regulatory scrutiny.

  • GPT 5.5 Release: OpenAI has launched GPT 5.5, designed specifically for "real work" and powering agents.
      • Capabilities: Excels at using tools, checking its own work, and completing complex multi-step tasks across software environments.
  • Adversarial Distillation: Michael Kratsios (former U.S. CTO) issued a warning regarding foreign entities, specifically China, using "industrial-scale" campaigns to distill American frontier models.
      • The Threat: Using tens of thousands of proxy accounts to extract model capabilities, effectively stealing billions in R&D value at the API level.
  • Industry "Slop": Discussion of "Choppelgangers"—a new slang term for lower-quality, distilled versions of frontier models, often from Chinese open-source labs.

Market Intelligence & Corporate Restructuring

  • Medallia Writedown: Thoma Bravo is reportedly handing the software firm Medallia over to creditors, resulting in a $5.1 billion equity wipeout.
      • The Cause: High debt loads ($3B) and concerns that AI will supplant its customer feedback services.
  • Intel Earnings: Intel stock rose 15% after-hours following a Q1 beat and strong guidance driven by data center sales.
  • Meta & Microsoft: Both firms are leaning out, with Meta cutting 10% of its workforce (8,000 employees) and Microsoft offering early retirement to approximately 7% of its staff.
  • Bob Iger: Reported to be joining Thrive Capital following his tenure at Disney.
"The customer does not care if you’re fully autonomous. Customers care if the work is done... If you’re getting the job done every single time and you’re profitable, the customer could not care less." — Victor Boyd, CEO of Cavalla
"Wall Street only covers the mega and large-cap stocks. We use LLMs to generate high-quality research on every single U.S. stock, including the ones that Wall Street doesn’t cover." — Nick, Founder of Everyticker
"There is a big anti-fraud moment in the United States... $600 billion a year is being stolen by people contracting with the government. We need to restore trust in our institutions." — Alex, Co-Founder of The Antifraud Company

When you're talking about a hundred million dollar training run and a Chinese lab can distill it and sneak the weights out and exfiltrate the data, that's a lot different of an economic impact from stealing something that I don't know, cost billions to train or billions to put together. So, good news there, hopefully they're successful. It was also sort of a white pill to see a lot of the labs working together to understand, "Hey, we're seeing this weird amount of data go to this particular company, are you seeing the same thing?" "Or it's a shell company and we have five shell companies in these areas that are asking for these queries." And then the other lab says, "Oh yeah, we're actually getting something similar." And if you puzzle piece those together, all of a sudden you're getting a really solid map of what a frontier system is capable of. And so it feels like we, the industry, are the victim of a distillation attack even if it might not register among a single lab because that single lab is only getting hit with like one-third or one-fifth of the total attack. And so LLMs really speed that up. It's sort of getting structured entities out of the unstructured data. And then we ontologize this, we have like this company has this contract, has this relationship with this employee, etc. We build sort of this ontology knowledge graph-like thing. And then we have the second layer, the rules model essentially. My co-founders are both lawyers, have a legal background, they've been working on sort of the fraud issue in the legal sphere and so they know what that looks like. And with their input and with their expertise we're able to essentially develop this rules model, this rules layer, compare that to what we're seeing in the real world and flag things that look like violations in real time, or at least stuff that could be violations and we need to get more information on. A lot of my interests in the past bit have been in the field of neurotech, which includes a focus in whole-brain emulation with the focus on doing this with worms first, but also like how useful this could be for brain-computer interfaces and understanding consciousness maybe in that sort of direction. So I mean, we've talked to a number of companies that are working on brain-computer interfaces bumping into this general scientific discipline. What was the decision to not join one of the existing efforts or stay in academia and actually go out on your own? Yeah, that's a good question. I think there's a lot of bets that people have to make and these bets are unfortunately kind of fundamental science bets of like, "Oh, is this physics going to work? Is this biological truth actually true?" So I think while these people are... I think all these bets are really interesting, there's no 100%. And so I think it kind of makes sense to instead make your own bet on what you think makes the most logical sense. That's kind of my reasoning here. Do you also think it's a better time than ever to be working more independently on biotech generally because of the advance of AI? We've talked to a couple companies that are sort of working on the AWS of lab equipment, more rental projects, so that maybe you don't need to raise a billion dollars out of the gate and build a biosafety level 2 lab on day one. So I think that's a really good time for you to do it right now. Help me bridge the gap between what we've seen in BCI with mostly reading from the brain getting an X and Y output so you can control a mouse on a computer—incredibly impactful technology—and then simulating nervous systems. How are these two things related in your mind? Why are they important to overlap? Yeah, yeah. So I think right now BCI technology is very powerful in the sense that it is on a great path towards clinical applications. Soon, people who are paraplegic can maybe walk again and people who are blind can see again. But a lot of this comes from almost like post-hoc problem solving. You throw in enough data at a model of someone's brain and maybe you can help them move their arm in the XY direction or move a mouse in a specific way. But when you are able to actually decode information from the brain and read and understand exact signals coming from every region of the brain, being able to truly control the brain—for example, if you know which regions to activate—then you have a more naturalistic control method. So like instead of only being able to move my arm left or right, you can move your arm very naturalistically in any degree of freedom. And I think that's kind of the power of being able to simulate the brain. You could do a lot of research to understand one of the most complex things in the universe. And I mean, obviously, we're not starting with the human brain, but any level tells you the sort of data that you need. Like, do I need voltage data or is calcium data enough? Is light sheet or electron microscopy enough? So there's all these questions the C. elegans can answer, and once we answer that question, obviously the goal is to move on into zebrafish and mice and fly and so on. But right now we are not close to mouse, for example, because there's no way to image the mouse brain while the mouse is still alive. But you can with C. elegans. Fascinating. Tim Cook is stepping down. What happens to Apple now? | Equity Podcast

TechCrunch(668.0K) ·35:55 ·104 views · 2026-04-24
Tim Cook (Former CEO, Apple) John Ternus (Incoming CEO, Apple)
Apple enters a new era as Tim Cook prepares to step down in September, while the broader tech landscape is defined by "circular" multi-billion dollar AI deals and a projected resurgence in high-profile IPOs for 2025.
TL;DR Apple enters a new era as Tim Cook prepares to step down in September, while the broader tech landscape is defined by "circular" multi-billion dollar AI deals and a projected resurgence in high-profile IPOs for 2025. The "Circular" Investment Model: Amazon has committed...

TL;DR

Apple enters a new era as Tim Cook prepares to step down in September, while the broader tech landscape is defined by "circular" multi-billion dollar AI deals and a projected resurgence in high-profile IPOs for 2025.

The Anthropic-Amazon Mega-Deal and AI Rivalries

  • The "Circular" Investment Model: Amazon has committed an additional $5 billion to Anthropic (totaling $13 billion), while Anthropic has committed to spending over $100 billion on AWS over the next decade.
      • Market Skepticism: The hosts noted that these deals often act as "circular revenue," where cloud providers invest capital that is immediately returned as infrastructure spending.
  • Anthropic’s "Mithos" Leaks: Reports indicate a hacker group gained access to Anthropic's Mithos model, suggesting the "contained" safety-first model is more exposed than previously thought.
  • OpenAI vs. Anthropic: OpenAI CEO Sam Altman criticized Anthropic for "fear-based marketing." The panel noted the irony of "the pot calling the kettle black," as both companies use safety concerns to drive market positioning while operating as profit-driven entities.

SpaceX’s Strategic Pivot to Cursor

  • The Cursor Acquisition Option: SpaceX has partnered with Cursor to develop next-gen AI, including a $60 billion option to buy the company later this year.
      • $10B Kill Fee: The deal includes a massive $10 billion breakup fee, effectively locking Cursor out of the market for competitors like Microsoft.
  • The Failure of xAI’s Grok: The move is seen as an admission that xAI’s Grok is failing, particularly in coding. Reports suggest even xAI employees were not using Grok for development.
  • IPO Considerations: The acquisition is structured as an "option" likely to avoid paperwork delays as SpaceX moves toward its own IPO.

2025: The Return of the IPO Market

  • SpaceX and "Confidential" Filings: Despite being confidential, details of SpaceX’s filing are leaking, likely because the company is engaging almost "every bank in the world" to set market expectations.
  • Revolut’s Fundamentals: The fintech giant is eyeing a $200 billion valuation. Unlike many "vibes-based" AI startups, Revolut shows strong fundamentals:
      • Revenue: $6 billion in 2025 (projected/reported), up from $4 billion.
      • Profitability: A net profit of $1.7 billion and a recently secured UK banking license.
  • Cerebras Infrastructure Play: The AI chipmaker is preparing to go public with a $23 billion valuation. While it has real revenue in the hundreds of millions, its valuation remains "stratospheric" based on future AI hype.

Apple’s Transition: The Move to John Ternus

  • Leadership Change: Tim Cook will step down as CEO in September 2024, moving to Executive Chairman. John Ternus, currently head of hardware, is the heir apparent.
  • Operations vs. Product: Cook’s legacy is defined by "operations as a product"—mastering the China supply chain and services revenue. Ternus is viewed as a "product guy," signaling a potential return to the Steve Jobs-era focus on hardware innovation.
  • The App Store Challenge: The new leadership faces intensifying legal and regulatory pressure regarding the 30% "Apple Tax."
      • Historical Context: Cook was known for being "ruthless" with developers (e.g., the famous sit-down with Uber’s Travis Kalanick), but the panel questioned if Ternus would maintain the same level of aggressive marketplace control.
  • Political Shielding: As Executive Chairman, Cook is expected to remain the primary liaison for government relations, specifically navigating the complexities of a potential second Trump administration.
"This is very like pot calling the kettle black reinvented from first principles... it is a reminder that Anthropic, even though maybe different from OpenAI, is kind of indulging in that same language." — Sean O'Kane
"Tim Cook’s operations strategy is an Apple product and it changed total economies... The question is what happens when an operations guy leaves? You can make great products, but you need an operations strategy." — Kirsten Korosec
"Often when we write about these investments, it's framed as a bet. It feels like this has become a market where everyone is betting on everything." — Anthony Ha

Intel Delivers Strong AI-Fueled Outlook | Bloomberg Tech

Bloomberg Podcasts(472.0K) ·44:36 ·37 views · 2026-04-24
Lip Bu Tan (CEO, Intel) Ed Ludlow (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
Intel has reached a historic stock record by surpassing its 2000-era peak, driven by a resurgence in CPU demand for AI servers, while Big Tech giants Meta and Microsoft are executing massive layoffs to offset surging AI infrastructure spending.
TL;DR Intel has reached a historic stock record by surpassing its 2000-era peak, driven by a resurgence in CPU demand for AI servers, while Big Tech giants Meta and Microsoft are executing massive layoffs to offset surging AI infrastructure spending. Market Milestone: Intel...

TL;DR

Intel has reached a historic stock record by surpassing its 2000-era peak, driven by a resurgence in CPU demand for AI servers, while Big Tech giants Meta and Microsoft are executing massive layoffs to offset surging AI infrastructure spending.

Intel’s "Glory Days" Return

  • Market Milestone: Intel shares surged to a record high, surpassing their dot-com era peak with a year-to-date gain of over 120% in 2026.
  • CPU Resurgence: Bloomberg’s Ian King notes that general-purpose CPUs, once considered "backup singers" to Nvidia's accelerators, are now essential for AI servers.
      • Supply Constraints: CEO Pat Gelsinger reported that demand for CPUs currently exceeds Intel's ability to supply.
  • Foundry Business Progress:
      • Process Milestones: Intel is seeing 6–7x production increases in its 18A process.
      • Strategic Position: Benchmark analyst Cody Acree raised his price target to $105, arguing Intel is the only viable US-based alternative to TSMC.
      • Advanced Packaging: CFO David Zinsner highlighted that packaging has grown from a "hundreds of millions" opportunity to a multi-billion dollar business.

The Human Cost of the AI Pivot

  • Massive Job Cuts: Meta and Microsoft are cutting or buying out approximately 23,000 roles combined.
      • Meta: Planning to trim 10% of its workforce (8,000 layoffs and 6,000 unfilled roles) to offset a projected $135 billion in capital expenditures (CAPEX).
      • Microsoft: Offering voluntary buyouts for the first time, potentially reducing its US workforce by 7% as data center costs mount.
  • Margin Maintenance: Analysts suggest these cuts are designed to keep margins flat rather than boost them, as companies shift from high-margin software sales to lower-margin GPU infrastructure.
  • Sector-Wide Trend: Oracle also recently cut thousands of jobs, signaling an industry-wide "milking" of legacy cash-cow businesses to fund AI build-outs.

AI Infrastructure: Energy and Defense

  • X Energy IPO: The nuclear energy firm, backed by Amazon, launched its IPO at $23/share (opening near $28).
      • Data Center Power: CEO Clay Sell emphasized that safe, "meltdown-proof" small modular reactors (SMRs) are the key to making nuclear energy "economic, boring, and financeable" for the AI era.
  • Defense Innovation: Andreessen Horowitz (a16z) is backing UDC, a startup building autonomous underwater vehicles.
      • Strategic Importance: General Partner Aaron Price Wright highlighted the need to protect undersea fiber cables and maritime interests in conflict zones like the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Cost Disruption: UDC aims to produce units for $40,000–$60,000, compared to legacy defense systems costing $2 million–$5 million.

Looking Ahead: The $15 Trillion Earnings Week

  • Breaking News: Google is committing up to $40 billion in investment to AI lab Anthropic, starting with $10 billion in cash at a $350 billion valuation.
  • Crucial Calendar: Next week, tech giants representing $15 trillion in market cap—including Microsoft, Alphabet, Amazon, Meta, and Apple—will report earnings.
  • Investor Focus: Market attention is shifting from simple "AI hype" to a strict demand for Return on Investment (ROI) and clarity on future CAPEX spending.
"The CPU is hot again—this general-purpose product that was the backup singer to the AI accelerators from Nvidia is suddenly taking center stage."

— Ian King, Bloomberg Semiconductor Reporter

"Intel really is the only alternative to Taiwan semi [TSMC], and to bring that back into the US is a real strategic necessity for US manufacturing."

— Cody Acree, Equity Research Analyst at Benchmark

"Our submarine capabilities haven't really advanced in half a century. It costs billions of dollars and puts lives at risk... many of the things we need to worry about in the ocean today actually don't need manned crews."

— Aaron Price Wright, General Partner at Andreessen Horowitz

It's what we would call voluntary redundancy over in Europe or the UK—a buyout here. Is that what Microsoft typically does, or why are they going that route this time? They've never done that before. I mean, they've done their share of big layoffs, right? So they're certainly not afraid to do that. But I think Microsoft often tries to be seen as a bit maybe more of a compassionate big tech company. You see that in the way they communicate their environmental goals, for example. But I think it's all part of the same trend, right? Companies are feeling like, in this moment, their employees should be able to do more with less. I don't think that it's straight up, "Hey, AI is replacing people" quite yet, but I do think companies are saying, "Well, I think we could probably get by with fewer folks these days." Brody, a question for you that I have is we've talked a lot about entry-level jobs and how they're going away. Now we're thinking about bigger-picture, higher-up jobs. What is it going to mean for layoffs for more senior executives across tech companies? Where could they possibly go? Just quick, Brody, we've got to get going. It's a tough market. Nuclear energy firm X Energy has raised over a billion dollars in an upsized IPO. Shares of the company, which counts Amazon among its biggest backers, were priced in the IPO at around $23 a share. The indication is that they will trade way above that when they get going. X Energy CEO Clay Sell joins us from the NASDAQ. It's been about a year since I last saw you and sat down, and here we are: big IPO, just reflect on it. "It has—you know, it's really been great seeing you a year ago, Ed, at South by Southwest," Sell says. But we've really had a good three-year run at X Energy in terms of customer acquisition with Dow, Amazon, Centrica, and Energy Northwest. In terms of our regulatory achievements, we got our fuel facility licensed; it's under construction in Oak Ridge, Tennessee. We proceeded with our first license for our fuel plant in Texas; we expect to get that around the end of this year. So it's been a year of great progress and a great time for us to enter the public markets. What is the status of those customer relationships? One of the things that you and I went through is the reality of this technology and how quickly it gets built into the real world. What is the reality of it right now? Well, our first project comes online in the 2030s, but it'll be the first of many, many, many to come. And the key with our technology, as you'll remember, is it allows us to reinvent the way we build nuclear power plants—to completely change the way we build them. We start with a remarkably safe plant, the safest technology that's available. It's a meltdown-proof fuel. The plant is elegant; it's relatively simple, with less concrete, less steel, and fewer parts, and it's easier to build. And we have a backlog of over 140 units between just our first three customers. And so that gives us a pathway to build and build and replicate, and we all know, you know, replication is the key to mastery. Replication—building the same plant over and over again—is the key to making nuclear incredibly economic, incredibly boring, and incredibly financeable. And that's what we're about at X Energy. You're about to start trading in a big IPO; don't make this boring, Clay. Just to update our audience, the IPO priced at $23 a share. What I'm seeing on the Bloomberg terminal is indications you're opening around $28 a share. We'll see how that goes. The chronology is important; I think the Dow project happens first, and then Amazon—is that correct? "Yeah, the Dow project will come first and be immediately followed by the Amazon project," Sell confirms. How real is the regulatory approval of this? The foundation—you and I have talked about the need to codify a lot of this. Do you have the support from the federal side of the government to get moving in the way that you want to? The policy support from the federal government has been key to launching this new era of nuclear. A key part of that was the reform of the Nuclear Regulatory Commission. Historically, you know, the NRC had a very bad reputation for being slow and cumbersome, with a lot of red tape making it very difficult to build new plants. But on a bipartisan basis, legislation has been passed to reform the NRC. Then President Trump came in and he put an amazing leader as the new chairman of the NRC, someone who has both the will and now the authority to move appropriately projects through the regulatory space. So again, we got our fuel plant license, and then we'll get our first power plant license, you know, by the end of this year in record time on both. Does the company yet have the license from the NRC for its design? We have the license to operate our fuel plant; we will receive the license to begin construction on our first power plant around the end of this year. Clay Sell, CEO of X Energy, priced this IPO at $23 a share, indicated to open at $28 a share. Thank you very much. The wars in Ukraine and now Iran have underscored the growing importance of autonomous aircraft and undersea vehicles. Andreessen Horowitz is backing UDC as a startup building autonomous underwater vehicles, pointing to a new frontier in ocean and defense tech. Aaron Price Wright is a general partner at Andreessen Horowitz. He just did that round in the last week or so and joins us on the show. I find this so interesting. I think over the course of the war in Iran, we focus a lot on drone technology and other missile technology, but with the Strait of Hormuz in particular, now that domain is becoming increasingly important. I think just explain your conviction and thesis with UDC as a case study. "Yeah, first thank you so much for having me. It's really wonderful to be here," Wright says. The ocean is largely unexplored and unconquered territory, and it's kind of remarkable how little innovation there has been in maritime over the last half century, despite how much it's grown in strategic importance for the United States and globally. I mean, if you think about the beginning of the conflict in Ukraine, the first thing that Russia did was cut Ukraine's undersea fiber so that they didn't have any internet access. You just mentioned we're seeing this again in Iran with the strait and how much global tension and conflict that's caused. And expect something similar if we were ever to be in conflict in the Pacific theater with the South China Sea. So, you know, really this was a market and an area very much hungry for innovation. If you look at our submarine capabilities, they haven't really advanced in half a century. It costs billions of dollars and these are people who are putting their lives at risk. Many of the things that we need to worry about in the ocean today actually don't need manned crews to be able to accomplish really important missions. Whether it's on the commercial side from inspecting oil and gas, or deep sea mining, or offshore wind, or other assets through to the defense space—a lot of those use cases don't need manned crews. I just want to point out Aaron, if you bear with me, that right now the story in public markets is stocks are higher, technology stocks in particular, on hope and optimism. Over this weekend, there will be peace talks between the United States and Iran. And we're here principally to talk about private markets, but I think there's been a bit of a disconnect in understanding something that's happening live in real time—a conflict like the one in Iran—and how that's given momentum to the industries and areas that you want to invest in. Has it got people moving? "Yes, it's a really, really good question, and it has," Wright says. I think it's been a real forcing function. It's put a magnifying glass up to our defense space and how ill-prepared we would be for a major conflict, and it's helped speed up a lot of the procurement processes and conversations with the Department of War. We're seeing the Department of War come to our early-stage companies and say, "How fast can you deliver a hundred? How fast can you deliver a thousand? How fast can you deliver ten thousand?" They are really starting to change their thinking around what the requirements are, what matters, and how do we make sure that we have the production scale capacity to be able to fight in a future conflict. So I'm very hopeful for peace in the Middle East, and also this has been a real wake-up call for our defense department—for what we really have to do and how we have to support the defense industrial base in order to be prepared for a future conflict. You know, even in the domain of the ocean, Anduril is an interesting case study as well, right? They have focused a lot, not just on the technology and autonomous vehicles in the water, but where they're placed around the world. But one data set—our Bloomberg tech producer Zoe Thomas always tracks the declassified spends—shows that where that dollar goes, it still goes to Lockheed Martin, even in the aquatic context. Is that the same story—that even if Anduril and UDC do move fast, the legacy primes are getting all of the government spending? Well, let's take Ulysses as the example. They didn't start the company as a defense prime. They started the company where the first use cases were seagrass planting, seafloor restoration, energy inspection, and port inspection. Essentially, they were working on use cases that had very strong cost curves where their customers couldn't pay more than 40, 50, or $60,000 for a unit to make the economics work. So they had to really focus on high-quality engineering to build a very low-cost and highly manufacturable, scalable product for their commercial customers. And you know, they have lots of operational experience in the field fielding these units for real use cases in the ocean. We're talking 40, 50, or $60,000 a pop for their UUVs. The Defense Department comes and sees that and they're like, "You've got to be kidding me. We're paying for the exact same thing, and we're paying two, three, four, or five million dollars a pop. Are you telling me I can buy this for $40,000?" So they have work to do to catch up in terms of proving out their tech in real missions. But when you're talking about two or three orders of magnitude cost difference for virtually the same thing, I think it's just a matter of time before we see these defense tech startups start to catch up to the primes.
