Former Binance CEO Speaks: From Crypto's Richest Man to Prison

The Free Press(450.0K) ·65:57 ·601 views · 2026-04-24
Changpeng Zhao (Former CEO, Binance)
Former Binance CEO Changpeng Zhao (CZ) details his transition from the head of the world's largest crypto exchange to a federal inmate, addressing his $4.3 billion DOJ settlement, his controversial presidential pardon, and his new focus on global education philanthropy.
TL;DR Former Binance CEO Changpeng Zhao (CZ) details his transition from the head of the world's largest crypto exchange to a federal inmate, addressing his $4.3 billion DOJ settlement, his controversial presidential pardon, and his new focus on global education philanthropy. ...

TL;DR

Former Binance CEO Changpeng Zhao (CZ) details his transition from the head of the world's largest crypto exchange to a federal inmate, addressing his $4.3 billion DOJ settlement, his controversial presidential pardon, and his new focus on global education philanthropy.

Legal Accountability and the US Prison Experience

  • DOJ Guilty Plea: CZ and Binance pled guilty to violating the U.S. Bank Secrecy Act (BSA) due to an ineffective anti-money laundering program during the company’s early growth phase (2017–2019).
      • Financial Penalties: Binance paid a $4.3 billion fine, while CZ personally paid $50 million and stepped down as CEO.
      • Addressing Terrorism Allegations: CZ maintains that while compliance was "weak" and "inexperienced," the court found no evidence that Binance actively or intentionally facilitated terrorist financing.
  • Incarceration Details: Because CZ is not a U.S. citizen, he was ineligible for a minimum-security "camp" and served four months in a higher-security correctional facility in Long Beach, California.
      • Prison Etiquette: He hired "prison consultants" (ex-wardens/guards) to learn survival strategies, such as never cutting in line and managing group dynamics to avoid extortion.
      • Richest Inmate: Despite his net worth, CZ lived among the general population, teaching fellow inmates and some guards about cryptocurrency.

The Trump Pardon and Political Allegations

  • The Pardon Context: CZ was pardoned by President Trump in April 2025. He attributes this to Trump’s broader pro-crypto stance, noting similar pardons for Ross Ulbricht (Silk Road) and Arthur Hayes (BitMEX).
  • Denying Quid Pro Quo: CZ firmly rejected allegations that his pardon was linked to Binance’s dealings with the Trump family's crypto venture, World Liberty Financial.
      • USD1 Stablecoin: While Binance listed the Trump-affiliated USD1 stablecoin, CZ claims the decision was driven by an Emirati investment firm (MGX) choosing it as a transaction medium, rather than a direct deal with the Trumps.
      • Personal Relationship: CZ states he has never met, messaged, or spoken with Donald Trump, characterizing the pardon process as a "black box."
  • Conflict of Interest: CZ argues that Trump’s crypto ventures are no more a conflict of interest than a president holding stocks, as broad pro-crypto policies benefit the entire global industry rather than just one family.

The Future of the Crypto Industry and Regulation

  • Global Adoption: CZ identifies the U.S. as a potential global leader in crypto if the current pro-innovation pace continues, though he highlights the UAE (specifically Abu Dhabi) as a strong competitor.
      • State Reserves: He cites Bhutan (mining with hydropower) and El Salvador (holding Bitcoin) as successful examples of nation-state adoption despite market volatility.
  • The "Meme Coin" Phenomenon: CZ views the rise of meme coins as a byproduct of aggressive SEC enforcement against "utility" projects; he believes they should remain a small, speculative part of the ecosystem rather than its focus.
  • SBF and FTX: CZ avoids judging the fairness of Sam Bankman-Fried’s 25-year sentence but challenges the narrative that "no one lost money," noting that creditors are being paid back at 2022 prices rather than the significantly higher current market values.

Binance Strategy and Philanthropy

  • IPO Potential: While CZ no longer manages Binance's daily operations, he notes that an IPO remains a viable future option for the company to gain credibility and traditional capital, though no immediate plans are in place.
  • Giggle Academy: CZ’s primary focus is now a free, gamified basic education platform for children (ages 2-6 currently), which currently serves 240,000 students.
  • Wealth Distribution: CZ intends to give away 90% to 99% of his wealth, though he admits that "giving money away well" is a difficult task requiring significant time and management.
  • Satoshi Nakamoto: When asked about the identity of Bitcoin's creator, CZ speculates it was most likely the late Hal Finney, citing the fact that the original coins have never moved.
"There's no police force in the world that's 100% effective... we agree that our earlier compliance program was weak... we were inexperienced, we thought we were good. Later on, we said, well, we could have done better." — Changpeng Zhao (CZ)
"The president, the most powerful person on the planet, he's going to make a lot of impactful decisions... why is it only in crypto that his family... having some holdings in crypto companies [is seen as] a conflict of interest?" — Changpeng Zhao (CZ)
"I had a very happy childhood... [with] dirt floors, no running water, no electricity. When you don't have something, you don't know what you're missing... I'm still a little child inside." — Changpeng Zhao (CZ)
Some of you may already know the name, Changpeng Zhao, or CZ, as he likes to go by. He’s almost universally known in the tech and finance worlds as crypto’s richest man. Forbes calculated his net worth as greater than $110 billion, which puts him ahead of Bill Gates, though CZ denies the Forbes estimate. He amassed this wealth by founding Binance, the world’s largest cryptocurrency exchange, transforming it from a startup in 2017 into one of the most powerful companies in finance. Then things took a turn. In November 2023, CZ pled guilty to violating the US Bank Secrecy Act by failing to maintain an effective anti-money laundering program. He stepped down as CEO and personally paid a $50 million fine, while Binance paid a $4.3 billion fine. When I asked him about this charge, he acknowledged that Binance’s earlier compliance program was weak, and that Binance could have done better. In all, the case led to a four-month sentence at a correctional facility in Long Beach, California, where he taught fellow inmates and even some guards about cryptocurrency. He also spent his time writing his newly released memoir, Freedom of Money, a memoir of protecting users, resilience, and the founding of Binance. In our conversation, I ask him about this DOJ case against him and Binance, and the controversy surrounding his pardon, which was issued by President Trump about a year later, and the alleged business dealings with the Trump family. I ask what he thinks about President Trump profiting from crypto ventures, whether he thinks it's ethical for an incoming president to launch a meme coin, and if he thinks Sam Bankman-Fried, whose firm Binance once invested in, was treated fairly in US courts. I also get into why terrorist organizations and criminal networks are drawn to cryptocurrency, and whether there's really an effective way to prevent that kind of illegal activity. We ended by discussing the possibility of a Binance IPO, who he believes Satoshi Nakamoto really is, and why CZ describes himself as pretty happy growing up in rural China, in a home with a dirt floor and no running water. As I was reading the book, he seems just as happy in that phase of his life as he is today. I learned so much from this conversation, and I think you will too. Stay with us. CZ, thank you so much for being here. Well, thank you for having me. You were born in rural China, and now you have more money than Bill Gates. And what’s so interesting about all of this is that a lot of people don’t even know who you are—people who aren’t cryptoheads, for example. And so I want to start at the very beginning. Tell us a little bit more about the family that you were born into. Yeah. Well, first of all, I don't think I'm richer than Bill Gates. I think the calculations by Forbes is wrong, but anyway. Yeah, I grew up—I was born in a small village and my mom is a math teacher, a high school teacher, and then my dad is an electrician. I grew up in a, you know, village environment in China before I was 10. We had like dirt floors, no running water, no electricity. I did some of my elementary homework under an oil lamp. And then I moved to a slightly bigger city called Hefei. And then at 12 I went to Vancouver, and then I did college in Montreal. And then I worked in Tokyo, then New York, then Shanghai. And then in 2013 I found Bitcoin, and then four years later we launched Binance. And we got lucky; Binance grew pretty big. Yeah. To recap what you just said, you moved from rural China to Shanghai to Vancouver. One thing you didn’t mention that I learned in the book is that you worked at a McDonald’s, you worked at a gas station. You end up on the Tokyo Stock Exchange, Wall Street. That’s already an evolution in and of itself. But then in 2013, your life really changed when you discovered crypto. What was it about crypto that made you want to basically turn your life upside down to pursue this budding industry? Yeah, for me crypto was like the technology that increases the freedom of money, right? So if internet increased the freedom of information—and I, you know, when internet happened, I was just in college, out of college around 2000. So I was too young to take advantage of it. And then I thought, you know, this, crypto, blockchain is the same order of magnitude of technology shift as internet. So I was like, "Okay, I'm not going to miss this, I want to get in early." So I sold my house, sold my little apartment, quit my job, and then went all in on crypto. I just understood it as a fundamental technology that will change everything, similar to AI today. At the time—like, this is what, in 2013, 13 years ago—I thought the next technology that comes along maybe 15 years later. Now it's AI, right? So I think these three—internet, blockchain and AI—are the three fundamental technologies in my adult life so far. So at 40, you founded Binance. It made you a billionaire. It became the world’s largest crypto exchange. Big picture, tell us what it was about you and your team that made Binance so successful. Yeah. So I think basically, fundamentally, what we did differently is we protected users really, really well. I think before us, there were many, many crypto exchanges. Even afterwards, there were many, many crypto exchanges. But because we're in the crypto industry, we deal with money, there's a lot of quick money. So many people are kind of attracted by the short-term returns and they try to keep money for themselves, and they sometimes don't protect users as well as we do. So we do many, many things in benefit of our users, sometimes at the short-term cost to us. But we earn enough fees so that we are sufficiently funded. And then we just grow really big because we actually make less money—because we make less money per transaction and we protect our users in a lot of instances where we have to put up the money. But we’ve grown really big in terms of user base. And when the user base grown really big, we benefited from the scale. So I think many people just didn't have this equation in their head. They always trying to get a little bit more from each transaction. We're like, "No, let's get a little bit less from each transaction and then have scale." So I think that was one of the biggest things. There's many other things where we got a bit lucky on. We started in the sort of ICO boom year in 2017. And then we had a strong team that worked really, really hard and they executed well. And ICO for listeners, it’s like an IPO but for a coin. I just say that because a lot of people that listen are not super familiar with crypto terms and things. But it is true, like in reading your book, I was fascinated by some of the anecdotes of like, you know, typically if people send crypto to the wrong address, it’s gone for them. And I thought it was really interesting to read about how your team took bespoke care to try to help people even though it was so expensive and did nothing for your business. So some of those anecdotes I thought were really interesting. I want to get into some of the most interesting parts of the book. And I think one element of the book that will, you know, have a lightbulb moment for people who don’t follow crypto so closely are some of your anecdotes in and around Sam Bankman-Fried. Of course, Sam Bankman-Fried founded FTX, which was another exchange, an exchange that competed with Binance. And you met Sam Bankman-Fried in 2019. In January 2022, my understanding is that FTX was valued around 32 billion. By November of that same year, it was on a path to insolvency. And he calls you asking for a couple billion dollars, really casual. He suggests selling you FTX. Ultimately, you don’t buy it. The exchange crashes. I think people will remember how shocking that was. And he’s charged with fraud and now he serves 25 years in jail. And I’m wondering, you know, what’s interesting to me in all of this is that 25 years. Do you think the law was fair on Sam Bankman-Fried or do you think it went too far? I, this is a very hard one for me to comment. Based on public information that I know, he took somewhere between $4 to $8 billion of user money and made investments, used that to buy tokens himself. And he did not disclose that to the public. I only learned that much, much later, right? So how you classify that, and then he lost most of the money and then, you know, there was a liquidity crunch—people could not withdraw. So I yeah, so I I'm not in a position to comment on whether the sentencing was fair or not, etc. Yeah, so it is what it is. It is what it is. I think some people wonder about it because it’s like, my understanding is no one lost money and most of the investors were not American, and so it sort of just questions like, you know, was it overreach? So yeah, well, on this point, I think when the statement that no one lost money is actually not necessarily true. I think based on US dollar terms, using crypto prices at the 2022 price, no one lost money. But back then, Bitcoin was what, 16,000, 20,000. If people held those crypto—if my understanding is they're compensating people based on 2022 crypto prices. If they're compensating people based on today's crypto prices, $70, $75,000, or at the high point, it was up to $125,000 per Bitcoin. My understanding is that's not the case what they're doing, right? So if you had crypto on FTX, you have one Bitcoin, now they gave you the Bitcoin at the 2022 price instead of today's price, which is 75k. So to say that nobody lost money, depending how how you calculated it, yeah. People lost the potential to make because of the misappropriation, is sort of what you’re saying. Yeah, yeah. So if people held their own Bitcoin until today, they would have they would have had a lot more money, yeah, than what they're getting from FTX. And also some international users—I don't know the details of this, but I read on Twitter that many of the Chinese users, for example, were not compensated because they said, I don't know, so this, I think there's still quite a lot of people who lost money, yeah. That’s a good point. As most people know, you had some legal issues of your own in November 2023. Binance pled guilty to violating anti-money laundering laws, which allegedly allowed money to flow to terrorists and cyber criminals. And, you know, it’s been almost three years since that chapter. You know, what do you think Binance looking back actually did wrong or could have done differently three years later? So both Binance and myself, we pled guilty to a federal charge, the Banking Secrecy Act. For Binance, I think there were more discussions about, you know, compliance programs, etc. And even though I was the CEO of Binance at the time and I handled some of the case negotiations. But my case was Binance violated the BSA law in the US, which basically says if you run a financial platform, you need to register in the US and you need to have a proper KYC program. So Binance.com, the global entity, did not register in the US. There's a separate arm called Binance.US that registered in the US. But Binance the global entity, when it first started before I think between 2017 and early 2019ish, they did have US users. So this is a fact, right? So for this part, they said, "No, you're violating the BSA law." And then at the time, the compliance program was not strong enough. But there were a lot of talks trying to say, "Look, Binance facilitated terrorist financing." That never happened, right? So Binance never actively, intentionally facilitated terrorist financing. Having a weak compliance program—you got to understand that no compliance program in the world is 100%. Even like the top banks, they will have some transactions that would be viewed as illicit or associated with bad actors, etc., right? So there's no police force in the world that's 100% effective. And then that's the part that was being debated and argued, etc. But there was no active—well, there were and there are no active, intentional facilitation of terrorist financing, etc. I think that's just a lot of the media trying to misquote some of the... there were allegations of it, but they were they were never proved. In fact, the court in my case—the court completely rejected the government's claim saying that there was no evidence for that charge. Well, I don’t think you, I don’t think anyone at Binance would want, you know, terrorists to get money, but should there have been more oversight, I think is like maybe some of the question that people have. And I wonder if you could go back in time if you would have done things differently in terms of building out or thinking about, you know, the oversight structure that was at Binance. Sure, sure. No, I think look, we we agree that our earlier compliance program was weak, right? We started as a technology company in Shanghai. And then two months later, six weeks later, we had to move out of Shanghai and then we were moving around different places. And we started as a technology platform, right? So and the platform grew really, really quickly. So our initial compliance program, which we did have from the early day—from the very, very early days we had KYC programs; we used Sumsub, we used Jumio to do KYC. But how strong they are, we were not perfect. So but at the same time though, people need to understand that there's there's no standard to say, "Look, you're compliant"—it's not a binary thing. There's no standard saying your compliance program is good enough, right? It's all subjective. So we at the time we were inexperienced; we thought we were good. Later on we said, "Well, we could we could have done better." So that's kind of the situation, yeah. You know, this sort of is tied up in this other conversation around some of the taboos around crypto. And I think some people ask like, is there really a good way to prevent this type of illegal activity? You know, because some might say, you know, one of the points of cryptocurrency is that it’s designed to facilitate decentralized transactions. The traditional media like to portray crypto as, you know, this place where all these illicit transactions happen. But this both percentage-wise... percentage-wise like there's a hundred times less transactions in crypto, less illicit transactions in crypto by volume. And then there's like probably 10,000 times less amount-wise. So there's a very common misconception. And I can tell you why. Crypto is very easy to trace, and people are very good at tracing it. Crypto is one single public blockchain, Bitcoin, right? So it's very—and it's permanent. It's always there. So anybody can look at it. People say it's pseudo-anonymous, but if you tie a KYC information from large exchanges, you can pretty much know every address. So people are very good at labeling labeling those addresses now. So it's actually extremely hard to do illicit transactions in crypto without people knowing. My follow-up question: you know, someone might say then why would the IRGC want payments from boats passing through the Strait of Hormuz to be in crypto? Or why would people choose crypto to send money to Hamas? Like if it’s, you know, like like why is crypto their choice of their vehicle to move money? So crypto is very easy to trace, but if you run your own crypto wallet, it's hard to block. So you can see it, but you may not be able to block it, right? So the IRGC, the Iran situation is like there's an open war. They said, "Look, we're just going to receive Bitcoin." I can tell you that the US government is tracking every single transaction to IRGC. So whereas if they do through banks, they actually they actually can't see it, right? So they have to subpoena to get information, there's a lot of different banks in the world. It's actually hard to get that information. Whereas on the blockchain, it's very very easy to trace. But if you use raw Bitcoin, if you have your own self-custody wallet and somebody else is sending from their own self-custody wallet, the network will not reject a transaction and there's no way for other people to block it. So you have sovereignty in that sense, but it's very—it's very traceable. So it’s very traceable, but who has the power to block it? Actually, for Bitcoin, if you don't use a centralized exchange, then no one. On the centralized exchange, then each operator—each platform—does have the power to block it. But if you just use native Bitcoin, then no one can block it. This opens up a really interesting philosophical question that I was thinking about, which is: you know, on one hand people say it enables these black market transactions, everything that we’ve been talking about, and people don’t like that. At the same time, there’s sort of this interesting contradiction, which is that, you know, one core philosophy, I think—you’ll correct me if I’m wrong—of crypto is that no government or intermediary should get to decide who is allowed to participate. You know, and I think maybe some people have tension with this idea of someone coming in to block, even if, you know, that payment, you know, would go to someone like the IRGC or like a terrorist organization regardless of their sanctioning. And I wonder how you think about that sort of contradiction between the ethos of, you know, maybe some of the early founders of crypto and some of like the... what centralized exchanges might be incentivized to do. There is a balance here. At certain times, you—we want law enforcement to be able to freeze funds to chase bad actors, etc. In the other extreme, I'm not sure if you want government... there are cases where bad actors, even in the law enforcement space or governments, are freezing people's funds unilaterally without good reason. And also and also if you think about it, right? You work very hard to make your own money, but the government can make you poor arbitrarily by printing more money, right? So let's say there's $100 in circulation. If the government prints another $100 and puts that in circulation, you just become poor by 50% without doing anything, right? So you work really hard for your money, you pay your taxes, and suddenly somebody makes you 50% poor. In some developed countries, this quantitative easing, inflation, printing some money is is sometimes useful, it's not always bad. But doing that excessively is bad, right? So there's many different governments in the world. Some governments do it very, very aggressively, some some governments don't. So whether that's good or not, everybody can decide for themselves. But with crypto, with Bitcoin, no, there's it's a fixed supply. So number one, no one's going to print more of that at will, right? So no one has the power. If you want to print more of that, you can go buy mining machines and there are less and less of that coming out. Is a Recession Coming? What 100+ Companies Are Telling Us | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(170.0K) ·31:43 ·6.6K views · 2026-04-24
Steve Eisman (Host)
> "The global financial system functions on Treasuries.
TL;DR: Q1 2026 earnings results from over 100 companies suggest that a recession is not imminent; however, high interest rates and geopolitical tensions are creating a "precarious" environment for software and homebuilders while disrupting the long-standing FICO monopoly. ...

TL;DR: Q1 2026 earnings results from over 100 companies suggest that a recession is not imminent; however, high interest rates and geopolitical tensions are creating a "precarious" environment for software and homebuilders while disrupting the long-standing FICO monopoly.

Macro Outlook and Private Credit

  • Recession Status: Broad earnings data indicates a resilient economy. While the war in Iran has increased oil prices—leading United and American Airlines to cut 2026 guidance—bank credit quality remains benign.
  • Blackstone (BCRED): The flagship $81 billion private credit fund saw performance flatten in March.
      • Write-downs: Portfolio value was dragged down by Medallia and Affordable Care, marked at 60.3 and 69.8 cents on the dollar, respectively.
      • Credit Quality: Non-accruals increased to 1.4% of fair value.
  • US Debt Crisis: Despite warnings from former Treasury Secretary Hank Paulson regarding the 125% debt-to-GDP ratio, Eisman remains unconcerned. He argues that there is currently no liquid alternative to the U.S. Treasury for the global repo market.

The End of the FICO Monopoly

  • Regulatory Shift: The FHFA announced that Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac will now accept VantageScore as an alternative to FICO for mortgage approvals.
  • Market Impact: This concludes FICO’s decades-long monopoly on GSE mortgage world scores. The stock has dropped over 40% this year; Eisman maintains a short position.

Industrial Growth and the "AI Tail"

  • GE Aerospace: Reported 25% EPS growth and a massive 87% increase in total orders ($23 billion), signaling that airlines are not yet pulling back on expansion despite geopolitical opaque-ness.
  • GE Vernova (GEV): A primary beneficiary of the AI data center boom.
      • Demand: AI centers are driving a 3% annual growth in US electrical production. GEV is one of only three global manufacturers of the gas turbines required to meet this demand.
      • Growth: EPS rose 85% year-over-year; orders reached $18.3 billion (up 71%).
  • Tesla: Eisman characterizes the stock as a "dream" play. While auto fundamentals are weak and the 2026 PE is over 200x, bulls are focused entirely on Elon Musk’s $25 billion annual CapEx commitment to autonomous vehicles and robotics.

Sector Turnarounds: Healthcare and Software

  • Managed Care: The industry shows signs of stabilization.
      • United Healthcare (UNH): Reported a beat ($7.23 EPS vs. $6.57 expected) driven by better Medical Loss Ratios (MLR), though issues remain in the Optum division due to new Medicare coding penalties.
      • Molina: Faces headwinds as the government prunes Medicaid rolls, leaving behind a sicker, more expensive cohort.
  • Software Risks: Eisman warns against "bottom fishing" in software.
      • ServiceNow: Despite 22% revenue growth, the stock fell 13% on news of delayed deal closings in the Middle East.
      • The AI Threat: Eisman believes AI will slow "seat growth" and erode the pricing power software companies have enjoyed for decades.

Housing and Interest Rates

  • Homebuilders: The 10-year Treasury yield hitting 4.4% has severely hampered the spring selling season.
      • D.R. Horton (DHI): EPS fell 13% year-over-year, but the stock rallied because results were "not as bad" as feared.
      • Meritage Homes: Trading below its tangible book value of $75. The company is pivoting from land acquisition to share repurchases (3% of shares retired this quarter).
"The global financial system functions on Treasuries. Banks park money overnight with each other through a multi-trillion dollar global repo market that only uses short-term Treasuries... If and when there is a large liquid alternative to Treasuries, I will become more worried about the deficit." — Steve Eisman
"The negative reaction to ServiceNow and IBM show how precarious building a positive software narrative can be. I know there are some of you out there who are looking to bottom fish here. I would tread very carefully." — Steve Eisman

Now before we move on to other earnings, I want to flag news on Fair Isaac, aka FICO. FICO is the backbone of the entire consumer lending industry. When a consumer applies for any kind of consumer loan, the lender, whoever that is, pulls a FICO score from Fair Isaac. That score is the largest determinant of whether the consumer gets the loan. FICO has had a monopoly on this process for decades. No longer. The FHFA, which is the regulator of Fannie and Freddie, announced this week that the GSEs will start accepting VantageScore loans from approved lenders immediately, as an alternative to FICO. This effectively concludes the era in which FICO Classic was the only approved score in GSE mortgage world. The updated loan-level pricing adjustment will present a level playing field between FICO and VantageScore. Previously FICO bulls argued that the pricing grids would favor the classic FICO score and they are wrong. FICO's stock has been under assault with the stock down over 40% this year. Full disclosure, I have been short FICO for a few months. I flag this story because in a few weeks we will post an interview with a sell-side analyst who covers FICO and the credit bureaus and we are going to examine this story in great depth. Now let's move on to health insurance. It looks like that for now the industry is turning, or at least appearing to turn. With respect to UNH, we have spoken about UNH for many months now. The company has been dealing with high medical costs as well as problems in its Optum division, a division that provides medical services to Medicare Advantage customers. And that means that UNH has the ability to reprice fairly quickly. It looks like that's what happened this quarter. UNH reported earnings per share of $7.23 versus $6.57 expected. So a nice beat. But I would just point out that earnings per share was flat versus last year. Revenue though was also a little bit better than expected. As we've discussed before, Optum is an aggressive coder and the new Medicare Advantage healthcare pricing system penalizes companies like Optum. But that's a negative story that may unfold more in the future. For now, investors will look at the improvement in the medical loss ratio and hope for continued improvement. I would be careful with the Molina stock. Continued Medicaid attrition means that the remaining participants are part of sicker cohorts, and that does not bode well for future earnings. Now the current estimate for Molina for 2026 is $5, which is down 54% versus last year. Investors are playing for a turnaround and a big jump in earnings in 2027. I'm just not yet convinced. Moving on to industrials. Let's start with GE. Now GE has split into three companies: GE Aerospace, GE Vernova, and GE Healthcare. This week we will be discussing GE Aerospace which is symbol as the old GE, and GE Vernova symbol GEV. First GE. GE manufactures jet engines, largely for commercial airlines. And that business tends to go in waves. There are periods where airlines are expanding and need more planes, followed by periods of retrenchment. We are in a period without question of expansion right now. And that's why GE has been reporting good quarters, and it did so again. Yes, the war is causing investors to worry about travel-related companies, but so far airlines are not pulling back from buying new airplanes. GEV. Full disclosure, I've owned this stock for a while. Before we discuss earnings let me explain the basic story. GEV has three businesses. It manufactures gas turbines for utilities. It has an electrification equipment business and a wind turbine business. The wind business loses money. The key is the gas turbine and electrification equipment businesses and this is one of the best AI-related stories out there. Prior to 2020 electrical production in the US was barely growing. Thereafter, because of reindustrialization and the creation of AI data centers, electrical production is now growing around 3% per year. Now that may not sound like much, but 3% off of the huge electrical base of the United States, trust me, is an enormously large number. And we are constantly hearing about AI data centers and their need for electricity. And how they are looking at nuclear and any other alternatives they can get their hands on. Regardless, electrical production in the United States can only grow with the help of gas turbines. And gas turbines are manufactured by only three companies in the world: GEV, Siemens, and Mitsubishi. And that, in a nutshell, is the GEV story plus the benefits reaped by the electrification equipment division. Now onto earnings. GEV reported earnings per share of $3.30 versus $1.78, a beat, and up a whopping 85% versus last year. Revenue beat as well. Even more importantly, orders were 18.3 billion, up 71% year-over-year. Orders taken today do not create revenue until years from now. It takes that long to manufacture gas turbines. Thus, given the growth in orders, the company has earnings visibility for many years. Because of the strong growth, the 2028 PE is 36 times, which seems more reasonable. Boeing. This has been a very problematic company for years. But things seem to be moving in the right direction. IBM posted earnings per share of $1.91 and revenue of 15.9 billion, both better than expected. Software revenue increased 11% to 7.05 billion, which was in line with estimates. But given the precarious nature of software these days, investors were hoping for more than an in-line software revenue result and the stock was down 7% after hours Wednesday. The negative reaction to ServiceNow and IBM show how precarious building a positive software narrative can be. I know there are some of you out there who are looking to bottom fish here. I would tread very carefully. Moving on to homebuilders. First DHI. Now homebuilders have been having a hard time. The year started with some hope as the 10-year yield declined below 4%. However, with the inflationary pressures created by the war, the 10-year got as high as 4.4%, thereby putting a real crimp into the all-important spring selling season. DHI results however were not terrible. Closings came in modestly below guidance but this was offset by slightly stronger gross margins. Company reported earnings per share of 2.24 versus 2.58 last year, so down 13%, but that was in line with expectations. And the company maintained its earnings per share guidance for 2026 of down around 10% versus 2025. In absolute terms these results are not great, but expectations for the sector are so low that DHI and the group rallied on these numbers. The hope is that the war will end and interest rates will come down. Meritage Homes reported, and here too the results were not great, but not terrible. Earnings per share of 82 cents was down 51% versus last year. That's bad. But orders declined only 5% versus last year, which is less bad. On the positive front, free cash flow was stronger than expected as the company has begun to dial back land spend in favor of increased share repurchases given the stock's below-tangible book value valuation. Meritage purchased 3% of shares outstanding during the quarter. Now I recommended Meritage in January and so far it has not worked out because the war caused rates to climb pretty quickly. I still like the stock because the company continues to grow tangible book value and the stock is valued at less than tangible book value of $75. Finally, Pulte Home also reported and here too the results were not great, but not a disaster. Earnings per share was $1.79, down 30% versus last year. But on the positive side, orders were actually up 3% versus last year. Clearly higher interest rates have hurt the spring selling season for the entire group. And now we turn to our two mailbags from two viewers. Our first question is from Canaan who asks: "Dear Steve, I've been an enthusiastic subscriber to the YouTube channel for a while, and I have learned an enormous amount through it, especially about macroeconomic trends and influential market forces that often don't make the front pages of mainstream newspapers. You've made me smarter and more financially capable, and I thank you for it. Financial news outlets have covered Hank Paulson's recent exhortation that the govt make a plan for a collapse in the T-note market, noting that a govt debt crisis could be far more impactful than crises in other markets. I try to avoid alarmism, but the 1BBB legislation has ballooned our projected deficit and severely impacted lower- and middle-class consumers at a time when White House foreign policy has also dealt blow after blow to America's global standing. Do you consider Paulson's warning well-founded, and if so, how might everyday investors/non-investing consumers position ourselves relative to it? All the best, Canaan." A very fair question. Ever since the great financial crisis there has been this weird competition to see who can predict the next financial calamity. And the latest competition has been the focus around private credit. Here Hank Paulson is focusing on the federal deficit and the high levels of debt-to-GDP in the United States. The federal debt-to-GDP level is at an all-time high of 125%, and some people are worried, like Paulson, that investors will just stop buying US Treasuries. Now Hank Paulson is chiming in on this one. But first of all what I would say is that with all due respect to former Secretary of the Treasury Paulson, he's not so good at predicting a crisis. On August 1, 2007, Paulson stated that "the market impact of the U.S. subprime mortgage fallout is largely contained and that the global economy is as strong as it has been in decades." Looking back, that's about as bad a prediction as you can get. But everyone makes mistakes, so let's focus on this prediction. It's not like Paulson is the first to make such a prediction. People have been predicting the same thing about the impact of the deficit for 40 years. 40 years is a long time to predict a calamity. Two points here. First, Japan's debt-to-GDP ratio is 240%, almost double the United States, and Japan's debt markets function quite well. Second, the price of this risk is interest rates. If the market was worried about the deficit, truly, truly worried, then the 10-year Treasury yield would be considerably higher. Instead it's been in a range of 3.9 to 4.5% for the last several years. The commentators of this calamity never seem to ask why their 40-year predictions have not occurred. And I think I have an answer for that. The global financial system functions on Treasuries. Banks park money overnight with each other through a multi-trillion dollar global repo market that only uses short-term Treasuries. When large institutions need to park significant money for any duration, they buy long-term Treasuries. Why? Because there is no alternative. At least not yet. If and when there is a large liquid alternative to Treasuries, I will become more worried about the deficit. Our second comment comes from Boris. This is a long email about the current debate surrounding software and I have edited his comments down for the sake of brevity. I thought Boris's comments were very interesting. And he starts with the following: "Thanks for the podcast - I've been listening for a while and I find it very useful. Quick background: I'm a computer scientist, spent ~10 years as a Product Manager at Google, then co-founded a startup in 2019 (acquired in 2022). Over the last 2 years, I went back to coding quite actively, because AI tools made it viable for me again. I use Cursor, Claude Code and Codere daily. I wanted to share a practitioner perspective on the recent AI / software sell-off (I do have positions in some names mentioned). Main Thesis: AI made software development much cheaper and more accessible - but people are over-extrapolating what that changes. B2C Software: B2C software is usually not that complex technically. The moats are elsewhere: network effects (WhatsApp, Reddit), marketplaces (Uber, DoorDash), content (YouTube, TikTok), brand. In B2C, AI coding unlocks a long tail of small products that were previously uneconomic. I'm already seeing many one-person businesses, but most won't scale into public companies. Megacaps will likely benefit the most, since user acquisition is the real bottleneck - my running joke is that VC money for B2C startups is going straight into Google and Meta pockets. B2B Software: 1. Internalization. There's a lot of talk about it, and I think it's overstated. Key constraints: TCO - building is easy, maintaining is not (patching vulnerabilities, updating dependencies, remaining compliant with regulatory changes, doing user support). Talent - AI helps a lot, but system design still matters. Many companies don't have people with sufficient skills and a required mindset. For example, many gyms use Exercise.com to run their business, and it's unlikely to be internalized. Risk - no CIO wants core systems like payments processing being "vibe-coded." Compliance - in regulated industries, software tools must pass audits and certification. Vendors amortize this cost across many customers; internal builds don't. Valid point though is that companies will require less seats due to automation, which undermines companies with per-seat pricing. I wonder how it will change going forward (units of work/pricing?). 2. Competition / New entrants. Lower dev costs cut both ways. Everyone benefits, not only newcomers. Incumbents can also ship faster, reduce development costs, and expand margins. It's not obvious this lower development cost structurally favors challengers. 3. Sales (this part is often missing in the discussion). For most software companies, the largest cost is sales & marketing, not R&D. Examples: Palantir - sales and marketing is ~49% of opex, Salesforce - 59%, Shopify - 41%, Amplitude - 55%, Elastic - 51%. AI-assisted coding doesn't really reduce sales costs, especially for large enterprise contracts. New entrants will face similar go-to-market expenses. Also, many large use cases are already captured by existing SaaS companies, so entry costs are structurally high. Agentic systems: they are very exciting for many reasons, but two issues are underweighted: agents quality evaluation, tuning and testing is hard and requires specialized set of skills, and their outputs are non-deterministic, i.e. the same input produces different results. That limits use in critical workflows (e.g. financial systems). I see this as an additional automation layer, not a full SaaS replacement. Small point on SBC: Under GAAP SBC is expensed at grant price, not market price. Example: grant of 1000 RSUs at $100 vesting evenly over 4 years results in GAAP expense $25k/year, even if the stock goes much higher in the meantime. Economically, the bigger issue for shareholders is dilution, not the accounting expense. Even there, grant sizes tend to adjust with stock price over time, since they're typically tied to cash compensation levels. Most companies disclose total outstanding SBC (shares/options) in their annual report notes. Curious how you think about this, especially the sales vs R&D angle. Regards, Boris." And here are my takeaways. The large incumbents I think, they'll survive this AI assault, but, and it's a big big big but, seat growth I think is going to slow, and the raising of prices that this industry has enjoyed for decades will just become much, much, much more difficult. So the long-term bull case of software was always increasing seats and prices, and I think a lot of that goes away or is questionable. As far as his comments on stock-based compensation, I just disagree. Yes, the companies of course disclose the stock-based comp. It is however a real cost. That is why GAAP accounting requires it to be expensed. The idea that you can pay someone in stock and not count that expense when you report earnings is to me just ridiculous. After all, all these companies deduct stock-based compensation from their taxes. They treat it as a real expense then. Howie Liu On How To Future-Proof Your Company

Forbes(2.1M) ·20:46 ·38 views · 2026-04-24
Howie Liu (Founder and CEO, Airtable)
> "When apocalypse is coming...
TL;DR: The current AI revolution is a multi-layered "SaaS apocalypse" happening at 10x speed, requiring leaders to abandon traditional management techniques in favor of hands-on technical immersion and "agentic" business models where tiny teams generate billion-dollar revenues. ...

TL;DR: The current AI revolution is a multi-layered "SaaS apocalypse" happening at 10x speed, requiring leaders to abandon traditional management techniques in favor of hands-on technical immersion and "agentic" business models where tiny teams generate billion-dollar revenues.

Fundraising and Financial Resilience

  • Capital Strategy: Airtable has raised $1.4 billion to date, maintaining over $1 billion on its balance sheet while generating $100 million in annual free cash flow.
  • Inflection Timing: Liu advises raising capital only during or immediately before a company inflection point. Raising during a "slog" is significantly more difficult and less favorable for founders.
  • Safety Cushions: A common mistake is raising only 6–12 months of runway. Liu recommends raising significantly more than needed to provide a buffer for errors and product iteration.
      • Example: In their first round, Airtable raised $3 million instead of $1 million to ensure they had several years of runway rather than just 12 months.
  • The "Money is Time" Philosophy: Capital should be viewed as a tool to buy the time necessary to reach the next major milestone without the pressure of imminent insolvency.

Navigating the "SaaS Apocalypse"

  • Multi-Layered Disruption: Liu argues that AI is not a single shift (like the move to mobile) but rather 3–4 simultaneous disruptions occurring at unprecedented speeds.
  • The Billion-Dollar Solo Founder: He agrees with Sam Altman’s prediction that agentic leverage will allow companies of 1–10 people to generate hundreds of millions or billions in revenue.
  • Structural Shifts: The very nature of how companies operate is changing; instead of visiting websites (e.g., Nike.com), customers will likely use agents to shop and interact with services.
  • Wartime Leadership: During this period of disruption, "business as usual" is a recipe for failure. Leaders must focus on winning the technological "war" rather than optimizing internal processes or engagement.

Airtable’s AI Pivot: From No-Code to Hyperagents

  • Product Evolution: Airtable began as a no-code app-building platform in 2013, moved into enterprise sales five years later, and is now returning to its roots as a Product-Led Growth (PLG) company powered by AI.
  • Omni AI: This is Airtable’s internal AI feature that allows users to build applications through natural language conversations rather than manual UI configuration.
  • Hyperagent: A new, separate product play from Airtable designed to allow users to build and deploy their own frontier-model agents (e.g., at the level of Claude/OpenAI) to run entire company operations.
  • Agentic Leverage: Liu emphasizes that agents are moving beyond "chat" to autonomous work, capable of completing tasks in 30 minutes that previously took humans days.

Strategic Advice for Executives

  • Stay at the "Edge": Liu asserts that a CEO's primary job today is staying current with AI developments. Traditional management tasks (one-on-ones, all-hands) are secondary to understanding the technological frontier.
  • Real-Time Information Sources:
      • X (formerly Twitter): Liu identifies X as the essential source for AI news, noting that major companies often release groundbreaking updates there via developer posts rather than formal press releases.
      • Technical Immersion: Leaders must personally experiment with tools like "Claude Code" or "Hyperagent" to develop the "vocabulary" of what is possible in an agentic world.
  • The Speed of Innovation: Traditional annual launch cycles (like Salesforce’s Dreamforce) are becoming obsolete because the technology changes too much within a single month to wait for a yearly event.
"When apocalypse is coming... you don't maintain business as usual. What matters is winning or losing the war. And I think the only way to do this in this moment is to actually get hands-on with the most frontier AI experience that you can." — Howie Liu
"I think it's going to be the default that you're going to have very, very small teams... that build enormously leveraged companies that generate hundreds of millions if not billions of revenue because they're able to be fully agentically leveraged." — Howie Liu
"If you do nothing else as a CEO except to go and stay focused on what's happening every single week and constantly be playing around with it... you couldn't do much better than that." — Howie Liu

Howie, I want to start with talking about fundraising. Sure. Who here out here's looking to raise some money? Couple million here and there. Howie, how much has Airtable raised? We've raised 1.4 billion. Okay, 1.4 billion raised. We've kept a billion plus on the balance sheet and we now generate over 100 million per year of free cash flow, so we can self-fund effectively. Wow. So for all these, all these future fundraisers in the audience, give me some of your VC fundraising tips and give me a warning, too. Okay, one is timing. I think there's moments right before or right during an inflection of a company that are optimal for raising, and basically you should only raise then. Every other time is a horrible time to raise because if you're slogging it out and there's not like a, you know, kind of a light at the end of the tunnel, you're not going to be able to convince investors to come in right then. So I think timing is everything, like either at the very beginning, like before you've, you know, kind of ground out, or like right when you're about to hit a corner or you're starting to see some inflection. Timing is everything. When were you finding, when was a good time for you in Airtable to raise? What was like the setting, the situation? Well, we didn't always do this correctly, which is why a lot of this is, you know, great, great advice in hindsight I wish I had gotten. We raised our first round basically a year and a half into working on the product. We had self-funded; it was myself and my co-founder, and, you know, we had a prototype. We were going to launch imminently. And I think the inflection at that moment was just saying, look, like, we've built this product. It works. You know, we have some very early alpha testers. No real traction, no real revenue, but, you know, we think that this is going to be big and here's some of the early proof points. If you're investing now, it's kind of on the promise that it's going to work, right? And then we did another one right after we launched publicly when we actually started getting some early traction. Still not a runaway success, but, you know, it was still at a point where you could see the, the emerging trend, right? I think you want to, you want to do it before you've overplayed your hand. Gotcha. And you mentioned you have a billion dollars in the bank still. So many people burn through their cash, burn through the runway. What is your secret for not spending it? You know, we, we probably burned like if I had to, you know, remember correctly, like a million plus per year, which is not nothing. But we made sure that we raised three million for our first round instead of we could have raised like a million or a million five, and that would have meant we had 12 months to figure it out, right? So I think just always anticipating like how much are you going to need to get to the next milestone with some extra safety cushion, right? I think too many companies go out, they raise like, you know, six to twelve months of runway, they burn it all very quickly, and then it's like you're giving yourself very little room for error. Gotcha. So time is money, but money is also time. Yes. I love that. And for everyone here like, you, you know, Airtable's gone through a lot of pivots, a lot of changes. Yeah, from a C to B company, sorry, from a B to C company to a B to B company... sorry, from a C to B company to a B to B company and to what you're building now. Give me a really quick summary on kind of the evolution of Airtable and also where you're going now. Yeah. So when we started Airtable back over 10 years ago, it was 2013, really the vision was let's make app creation accessible to everyone, right? So it's really powerful obviously to build apps, whether it's for your business, you know, it could be a CRM app, it could be inventory management, but we wanted to make that really, really easy. And in 2013, that was a really powerful and kind of novel concept, right? Like app-building software was kind of at its, you know, kind of prime, and yet it was very inaccessible. So we picked a really large market, went after it, spent two and a half years building the product before launching, very similar to Figma actually. And, you know, we, we basically started out with just a product that was great and end users adopting it on their own, pulling out credit cards. There was no sales team; it was a pure PLG, product-led growth company, right? So build a product, people, if they like it, they're going to come, they're going to share it. And it wasn't until maybe five years later that we started really seriously going into enterprise and saying, hey look, we're going to not just let companies adopt us and pull us into larger companies, but we're going to go and more aggressively go and sell this product into larger companies. So Fortune 500, we're going to do these big multi-million dollar contracts, get entire pockets of the org on the product. That looks very different as a business, right? Requires a different type of team, you know, leadership, approach to building product and go-to-market. And we're now actually kind of in a little bit of a pendulum swinging back to our roots, which is I think in the era of AI, the best products in my opinion in AI, whether it's ChatGPT or Claude or some of the newer products like Lovable, Replit, etc., they're all working because they're just great products and people love using them and they come in droves, right? It no longer takes many, many years to hit 100 million in revenue with the best AI products. If you can create a self-serve motion where people just experience the magic of your product immediately, you get really, really fast adoption. So we're also now kind of shifting back into this gear of how do we make just really great products powered by AI that are immediately valuable to people who come in and try it. As a chef, I know flavor doesn't begin in the kitchen. It begins on the land. And Westholme's nature-led Australian Wagyu is a story written in the landscape of northern Australia. That's W-E-S-T-H-O-L-M-E.com/M-E-I-L-I-N. Unlike other AI, Slackbot lives in Slack. It understands you, your team, and your work, so you can ask it for help with anything. You mention AI because when we did a story together years ago and your whole big pitch was, you know, no-code engineering and making it so simple that, you know, me, I could make some sort of app or some sort of special functional spreadsheet. Now AI comes along and it's changed the entire game on what it means to not, to no-code software. And you guys are on the forefront of kind of ditching the old way of doing it. I think what's very, very unique about this moment is it's not just one disruption happening, right? People think of AI as like one thing, like the shift from desktop to mobile computing or maybe like, you know, commerce to e-commerce, brick and mortar to e-commerce, you know, but it's not like that. I think it's actually like three or four different disruptions each equally big or larger than any of those other ones—the internet, computing, like desktop to mobile. And they're all happening in 10 times faster the time and they're happening in a very stacked way. So to be very tangible, our core business, like you said, is all about making app creation accessible. And the first version of that was very, you know, kind of UI and user experience driven. Like you know there's this SaaS apocalypse, you know, word that's been used, and I think it's not just accurate, it's actually underestimating the amount of disruption that's happening. Yeah, so, you know, there's, there's this great kind of like, parallel to, you know, when, when you're in wartime, like a lot of the like small optimizations of, you know, how do you keep the trains running, how do you make this 1% more efficient, how do you make everybody more engaged and more, more stable in the company, like all that stuff doesn't really matter. What are the new business opportunities that you could create? As a very simple example, like if you work in property management for instance, you could first start by figuring out how to apply agents to your role, but then you could start to think about like what is the future of property management look like and how could I maybe go out and start an entirely new property management firm. This firm would not only run all internal operations with agents, but maybe the interface to the tenants who want to request different services, have complaints, have like feedback, whatever, can all be done with agents as well, right? And so only by going and using agents at the frontier can you even begin to understand, like it's like having the vocabulary to describe what's possible, much like it'd be very hard to build a great internet business if you had never actually used the internet or weren't internet fluent yourself. That used to be unheard of, right? Like there were like maybe you could count on one hand the number of software companies that got to 100 million within less than a year let alone like within a few months, and now it's kind of becoming more commonplace. But that doesn't come from nowhere, right? Like it's not like everyone who wants to go out and create that much revenue can just do it because it's cutthroat, it's very, very competitive. So new opportunities are being, you know, created as, you know, kind of the, you know, as the world changes very rapidly, but you have to kind of be right at the right moment, right place, right moment, to be able to capitalize on it. So I think it's absolutely a time where, you know, all the traditional rules of like you gotta be patient, you gotta be slow, you gotta be like, you know, be deliberate, are kind of getting thrown out the window and it's both exciting and, you know, in a way like, you know, kind of very, terrifying, but I think the only way to make it through it is to lean in and not to kind of lean out defensively. Will Social Media Bans Actually Work? | Bloomberg Tech: Asia 4/24/2026

Bloomberg Technology(721.0K) ·22:59 ·566 views · 2026-04-24
Shery Ahn (Host, Bloomberg) Win Gatchalian (Senator, Philippines) Ravi Iyer (Social Psychologist)
Governments worldwide are shifting toward hardline age-based restrictions for social media to combat youth mental health crises and platform addiction, moving beyond parental controls to treat digital access with the same regulatory weight as alcohol and tobacco.
TL;DR Governments worldwide are shifting toward hardline age-based restrictions for social media to combat youth mental health crises and platform addiction, moving beyond parental controls to treat digital access with the same regulatory weight as alcohol and tobacco. The...

TL;DR

Governments worldwide are shifting toward hardline age-based restrictions for social media to combat youth mental health crises and platform addiction, moving beyond parental controls to treat digital access with the same regulatory weight as alcohol and tobacco.

The Global Momentum for Youth Access Bans

  • The Australian Model: Australia has pioneered legislation requiring social media companies to keep users under 16 off their platforms, treating digital access as a regulated substance.
      • Early data suggests success; Snapchat usage among targeted groups dropped from 34% to 20% following enforcement.
  • Regional Expansion: Inspired by Australia, countries including Indonesia and Greece are initiating similar under-16 regulations. In Southeast Asia, Malaysia and the Philippines are the most active in considering legislative bans.
  • Legal Shifts in the U.S.: Beyond simple age limits, U.S. states are pursuing litigation based on "negligent design." Courts are increasingly viewing addictive algorithms as a product liability issue rather than a protected free speech issue.

Addressing the "Digital Addiction" Crisis

  • Senator Win Gatchalian (Philippines): Proposing a total ban for children under 16, citing data that Filipino youth spend an average of 5–6 hours daily on social media.
      • Impact on Well-being: Gatchalian argues that "doomscrolling" and late-night usage deprive youth of sleep and replace essential physical socialization with weaker digital bonds.
      • Constitutional Justification: While critics cite free speech, Gatchalian maintains that protecting the mental health and well-being of the nation’s youth provides a legal basis for limiting access.
  • Ravi Iyer (USC Marshall School): Argues that social media functions via "displacement," where 45% of U.S. children admit it interferes with sleep and 40% say it impacts school activities.
      • Gamified Interactions: Platforms use features like "Snapchat streaks" to psychologically compel usage, making it difficult for children to disconnect even when they recognize the negative impact.
      • Account vs. Content: Iyer clarifies that these measures are "age limits" rather than "bans," allowing youth to view content (like YouTube or TikTok videos) while preventing the creation of addictive, data-driven accounts.

Limits of Enforcement and Algorithmic Design

  • Catherine Thorbecke (Bloomberg Opinion): Expresses skepticism that age limits alone can solve the problem, noting that tech-savvy youth often find workarounds.
      • The Root Cause: Thorbecke argues that "somebody’s birthday" isn't what makes a platform dangerous; rather, it is the platform design, such as "rage bait" and algorithms optimized for maximum outrage and engagement.
      • The "Big Tobacco" Moment: Regulators are now viewing big tech through the lens of historical public health battles, suggesting that the current momentum is the best opportunity in a decade to force comprehensive safety reforms.
  • Implementation Hurdles:
      • Tech Compliance: Major platforms are currently under investigation for non-compliance with existing restrictions.
      • Parental Fatigue: Experts agree that parents are too exhausted to manage controls across dozens of apps, necessitating government intervention to change industry norms.

Notable Quotes

"The physical interaction is more stronger, the bond you develop is more stronger than compared to digital platform... we can prove very clearly that social media is also creating a lot of mental health issues." — Senator Win Gatchalian, Philippine Senator
"This isn't speech, this is more akin to a law saying that you can't play your radio at 3 a.m. outside of your neighbor's house. It has something to do with speech, but it is not about speech. It's about the way that we let people interact with our kids." — Ravi Iyer, Managing Director, USC Marshall School’s Neely Center
"I think my main issue with age limits is really that they don't address the root cause of the harms. And that's the algorithms, that's platform design, that's these features that keep us endlessly scrolling... it's not really somebody's birthday that will make these platforms safe." — Catherine Thorbecke, Bloomberg Opinion Columnist
We're focusing today on the push to use regulation to protect young people from potential harms posed by social media. Australia has become the trailblazer for curbing youth access to digital platforms, treating the issue like alcohol or cigarettes. And it's an idea that's been gaining traction in other countries too. Social media giants are facing a reckoning as moves to curb its negative impact, especially on children, gain momentum around the world. In the US, Meta is facing mounting legal challenges over allegations that its social media platforms were created to be addictive. After a Los Angeles jury found Meta and Google negligent in the design and operation of their popular platforms in March, more US states are now discussing ways to limit young people on social media. Australia is leading the global push on that front. Last year, it forced social media giants including Meta, TikTok, Snap and X to keep users under 16 off their platforms. The global community is looking at Australia and saying, "Well, if Australia can do it, why can't we?" It's a profound reform which will continue to reverberate around the world. The Indonesian government has also initiated regulations aimed at removing under-16s from social media platforms. Greece will be doing something similar by the start of next year. In Southeast Asia, the Philippines and Malaysia appear to be the most active in considering similar measures to Indonesia's ban. And the movement is gaining global traction as more countries debate similar measures. The fate of the Australian experiment will provide important information to governments seeking to regulate social media. It's already encountering substantial implementation hurdles. Major tech platforms are under investigation for non-compliance, and reports say the restrictions are relatively easy for children to get around. There are also plenty of legal hurdles. Courts have rejected suits that targeted platforms for the content posted by users, but proponents of regulation have proven in court that it's the algorithm that's addictive and causes harm. And then there are concerns about impeding on constitutional guarantees on free speech. And I presume that at some point, there's also going to be a European momentum.
